<p>Instead, a much cleaner approach would be to make the checkbox not fill the entire cell, so that it only gets selected if you press directly on the "checkbox" part of it.</p>
<p>This behavior can be accomplished by putting your <code>JCheckbox</code> inside a <code>JPanel</code> and center it without stretching it. To do this, we can make the <code>JPanel</code>s layout manager a <code>GridBagLayout</code>. See how when using <code>GridBagLayout</code>, the inner content doesnt stretch:</p>
<p><img src="http://i.stack.imgur.com/QsSEU.png" alt="Layout managers"> <sup>(from <a href="http://stackoverflow.com/a/7181197/837703">this StackOverflow answer</a>)</sup></p>
<p>So now, if you click in the empty space surrounding it, you will be clicking on a <code>JPanel</code>, so you wont be changing the <code>JCheckBox</code>s value.</p>
<p>The code for your <code>CheckBoxEditor</code> turns out like this in the end:</p>
<pre><code>class CheckBoxEditor extends AbstractCellEditor implements TableCellEditor {
    private static final long serialVersionUID = 1L;
    private final JPanel componentPanel;
    private final JCheckBox checkBox;
    public CheckBoxEditor() {
        componentPanel = new JPanel(new GridBagLayout()); // Use GridBagLayout to center the checkbox
        componentPanel.setOpaque(false);
        checkBox = new JCheckBox();
        checkBox.setOpaque(false);
        componentPanel.add(checkBox);
    }
    @Override
    public Object getCellEditorValue() {
        return Boolean.valueOf(checkBox.isSelected());
    }
    @Override
    public Component getTableCellEditorComponent(JTable table, Object value, boolean isSelected, int row, int column) {
        if (value instanceof Boolean) {
            checkBox.setSelected(((Boolean) value).booleanValue());
        } else if (value instanceof String) {
            checkBox.setSelected(value.equals("true"));
        }
        if (isSelected) {
            setForeground(table.getSelectionForeground());
            setBackground(table.getSelectionBackground());
        } else {
            setForeground(table.getForeground());
            setBackground(table.getBackground());
        }
        return componentPanel;
    }
}
</code></pre>
<p>(Note that you cant be extending from a <code>DefaultCellEditor</code> anymore - in the code above, youre now having to extend from an <code>AbstractCellEditor</code> and implement a <code>TableCellEditor</code>).</p>
<p>I think that this version of your <code>CheckBoxEditor</code> does what you want - if you click in the empty space around the check box, nothing happens. The check box only becomes check if you click directly on it.</p>
<p>Also, by using a <code>JPanel</code>, you dont have to do any <code>MouseEvent</code> calculations and (to me, at least), the code looks much cleaner and its easier to see whats going on.</p>
<hr>
<p><strong>EDIT1:</strong><br />
I read your comment and I found a solution: Why not leave the <em>editor</em> as it is, but then just make a cell <em>renderer</em> that derives from the <code>DefaultTableCellRenderer</code>? Then, in your <code>CheckBoxEditor</code> use the same borders and backgrounds as the renderer.</p>
<p>This should achieve the effect you want (Ive moved common code into outer class methods so I dont have to repeat them):</p>
<pre><code>private static void setCheckboxValue(JCheckBox checkBox, Object value) {
    if (value instanceof Boolean) {
        checkBox.setSelected(((Boolean) value).booleanValue());
    } else if (value instanceof String) {
        checkBox.setSelected(value.equals("true"));
    }
}
private static void copyAppearanceFrom(JPanel to, Component from) {
    if (from != null) {
        to.setOpaque(true);
        to.setBackground(from.getBackground());
        if (from instanceof JComponent) {
            to.setBorder(((JComponent) from).getBorder());
        }
    } else {
        to.setOpaque(false);
    }
}
class CheckBoxEditor extends AbstractCellEditor implements TableCellEditor {
    private static final long serialVersionUID = 1L;
    private final JPanel componentPanel;
    private final JCheckBox checkBox;
    public CheckBoxEditor() {
        componentPanel = new JPanel(new GridBagLayout());  // Use GridBagLayout to center the checkbox
        checkBox = new JCheckBox();
        checkBox.setOpaque(false);
        componentPanel.add(checkBox);
    }
    @Override
    public Object getCellEditorValue() {
        return Boolean.valueOf(checkBox.isSelected());
    }
    @Override
    public Component getTableCellEditorComponent(JTable table, Object value, boolean isSelected, int row, int column) {
        setCheckboxValue(checkBox, value);
        TableCellRenderer renderer = table.getCellRenderer(row, column);
        Component c = renderer.getTableCellRendererComponent(table, value, true, true, row, column);
        copyAppearanceFrom(componentPanel, c);
        return componentPanel;
    }
}
class CheckBoxRenderer extends DefaultTableCellRenderer {
    private static final long serialVersionUID = 1L;
    private final JPanel componentPanel;
    private final JCheckBox checkBox;
    public CheckBoxRenderer() {
        componentPanel = new JPanel(new GridBagLayout());  // Use GridBagLayout to center the checkbox
        checkBox = new JCheckBox();
        checkBox.setOpaque(false);
        componentPanel.add(checkBox);
    }
    @Override
    public Component getTableCellRendererComponent(JTable table, Object value, boolean isSelected, boolean hasFocus, int row, int column) {
        super.getTableCellRendererComponent(table, value, isSelected, hasFocus, row, column);
        setCheckboxValue(checkBox, value);
        copyAppearanceFrom(componentPanel, this);
        return componentPanel;
    }
}
</code></pre>
<p>Then, you have to set the renderer along with the editor in your constructor:</p>
<pre><code>checkboxColumn.setCellEditor(new CheckBoxEditor());
checkboxColumn.setCellRenderer(new CheckBoxRenderer());
</code></pre>
<p>Heres a couple of screenshots comparing the two methods:</p>
<p>
<strong>Your original method:</strong> &nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; <strong><code>JPanel</code> <em>and</em> <code>JCheckBox</code> method:</strong><br/>
<img src="http://i.stack.imgur.com/NsSr9.png" alt="Old">&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<img src="http://i.stack.imgur.com/oy9Yk.png" alt="New"></p>
<p>I can barely see a difference :)</p>
<p>IMHO, I still think that just using the plain Java table APIs is cleaner than calculating checks based on mouse pointer positions, but the choice is up to you.</p>

<pre><code>class CheckBoxEditor extends AbstractCellEditor implements TableCellEditor {

        });

}

<p>Lets start with a basic concept of some kind of "address"</p>
<pre><code>public class Address {
    private String street;
    private String state;
    private String city;
    private String zip;
    public String getStreet() {
        return street;
    }
    public void setStreet(String street) {
        this.street = street;
    }
    public String getState() {
        return state;
    }
    public void setState(String state) {
        this.state = state;
    }
    public String getCity() {
        return city;
    }
    public void setCity(String city) {
        this.city = city;
    }
    public String getZip() {
        return zip;
    }
    public void setZip(String zip) {
        this.zip = zip;
    }
    @Override
    public String toString() {
        return "Address{" + "street=" + street + ", state=" + state + ", city=" + city + ", zip=" + zip + };
    }
}
</code></pre>
<p>It has simple methods for getting and setting the values of the object...</p>
<p>Now, we can create objects that are "addressable"</p>
<pre><code>public class Bank extends Address {
}
public class Customer extends Address {
}
</code></pre>
<p>Both the <code>Bank</code> and <code>Customer</code> inherit from <code>Address</code>, meaning that they inherit all the functionality of that class.</p>
<p>Now, lets create some methods which work on <code>Address</code>...</p>
<pre><code>public void updateAddress(Address address, String street, String city, String state, String zip) {
    address.setStreet(street);
    address.setCity(city);
    address.setState(state);
    address.setZip(zip);
}
public void formatAddress(Address address) {
    System.out.println(address.getStreet());
    System.out.println(address.getCity() + ", " + address.getState() + ", " + address.getZip());
}
</code></pre>
<p>Pretty basic, one updates the address details and one prints out a formatted form of the <code>Addreess</code>...</p>
<p>Now for the magic...</p>
<p>Lets create a <code>Bank</code> and <code>Customer</code></p>
<pre><code>Bank bank = new Bank();
Customer customer = new Customer();
</code></pre>
<p>Now, lets update there address details...</p>
<pre><code>updateAddress(bank, "Some street", "Some city", "Some state", "Some zip");
updateAddress(customer, "Other street", "Other city", "Other state", "Other zip");
</code></pre>
<p>And print them...</p>
<pre><code>formatAddress(bank);
formatAddress(customer);
</code></pre>
<p>But wait, how is that possible?!?  Well, thats polymorphism.  Both the <code>Bank</code> and <code>Customer</code> can be used anywhere that <code>Address</code> is expected, because they inherit from this common ancestor class, they have the ability to "behave" like <code>Address</code> does.</p>
<p>Now, normally, Id do all this through interfaces and abstract classes, and Id probably maintain a <code>List</code> of <code>Address</code> classes associated with each object, but this is a simple example to help demonstrate a point...</p>
<h1>Issues...</h1>
<p>Your setters are around the wrong way, instead of...</p>
<pre><code>public void changeStreet(String Street){
    Street = this.Street;
    System.out.println("yo");
}
</code></pre>
<p>it should be...</p>
<pre><code>public void changeStreet(String Street){
    this.Street = Street;
    System.out.println("yo");
}
</code></pre>
<p>The reason you are having problems with...</p>
<pre><code>if (action.equalsIgnoreCase("S")){
    easySave.changeStreet(Street);
} else if (action.equalsIgnoreCase("ST")){
    easySave.changeState(State);
} else if (action.equalsIgnoreCase("C")){
    easySave.changeCity(City);
} else if (action.equalsIgnoreCase("Z")){
    easySave.changeZip(Zip);
}
</code></pre>
<p>is because <code>Street</code>, <code>State</code>, <code>City</code> and <code>Zip</code> are undefined within the context you are trying to use them...</p>
<h1>Editing account...</h1>
<p>In order to edit an account, you would need to be able to select one, once you have it selected, you could then manipulate it...</p>
<pre><code>        } else if (action.equalsIgnoreCase("E")) {
            Account account = //... Some means by which the user can select the account
            System.out.println("Street(S)\ State(ST) \");

                String City = //...
                account.changeCity(City);
            } else if (action.equalsIgnoreCase("Z")){
                String Zip = //...
                account.changeZip(Zip);        
            }
        }

</code></pre>

<pre><code>123.45
</code></pre>

line  : 1
null
</code></pre>

Digit0 = 0 | {Digit}
Num = {Digit} {Digit0}*
Frac = {Digit0}* {Digit}
Pos =  0? "." {Frac} | {Num} ("." {Frac})?
PosOrNeg = -?{Pos}
Number = {PosOrNeg} | 0
</code></pre>');insert into answers values('December',28.0,1.0,1.0,'Yes',1194.0,'<p>Let me assume you have two resource files with same name "spring/label.properties" stored in two different jar files.</p>

<p>P.S. Like other people in this topic I would recommend using thread pool for this task.</p>
<p>P.P.S. Consider using java.util.concurrent FixedThreadPool (launches n threads, if there are more tasks they are waiting for free thread) or CachedThreadPool (if there are more tasks creates new thread, otherwise reuses existing sleeping threads).</p>
<p><a href="https://docs.oracle.com/javase/7/docs/api/java/util/concurrent/Executors.html#newFixedThreadPool(int)" rel="nofollow">https://docs.oracle.com/javase/7/docs/api/java/util/concurrent/Executors.html#newFixedThreadPool(int)</a>
<a href="https://docs.oracle.com/javase/7/docs/api/java/util/concurrent/Executors.html#newCachedThreadPool()" rel="nofollow">https://docs.oracle.com/javase/7/docs/api/java/util/concurrent/Executors.html#newCachedThreadPool()</a></p>');insert into answers values('December',29.0,2.0,0.0,'No',57.0,'<p>I am first assuming that the threads are native threads (not green threads for performance reasons).
There isnt really a performance consideration with passing references of objects into the thread
other than making the gc continually skipping the reference for clean up which is more efficient than
serializing/deserializing the object into the thread.</p>
<p>Long story short, you should avoid creating any unnecessary threads that exceed the hardware capacity if you know that the running threads have a high utilization rate (ie very rarely blocking on io/net/db/etc) otherwise you will force the cpu to perform a thread context switch which is very expensive.</p>');insert into answers values('December',29.0,3.0,0.0,'No',6.0,'<p>I would likely create a thread pool with a configurable size, which process a queue of person objects.</p>

<p>If there is IO within the process, you might be able to increase your thread pool size, or decrease it if over utilising the CPU.</p>
